Bob Hayford - Macroinvertebrate Sampling in Aquatic Ecosystem Health Assessment
Contains unread posts
Robert Hayford posted Sep 15, 2024 2:49 PM
Macroinvertebrate sampling is a critical method in assessing the health of aquatic ecosystems, widely utilized by aquatic ecologists for monitoring environmental conditions. As bioindicators, macroinvertebrates provide valuable insights into the ecological integrity of freshwater systems due to their sensitivity to various stressors such as pollution, nutrient loading, and habitat degradation. My research has focused on this methodology, particularly informed by a peer-reviewed article published in Freshwater Science (formerly the Journal of the North American Benthological Society), titled &quot;The Use of Benthic Macroinvertebrates in the Biological Monitoring of Freshwater Ecosystems: The North American Experience&quot; (Rosenberg &amp; Resh, 1993).

This article presents a thorough examination of benthic macroinvertebrate sampling as an evolving and indispensable tool in ecological monitoring. It discusses diverse sampling methodologies and emphasizes the importance of standardized procedures for ensuring the consistency and reliability of data collection across different studies. The authors highlight how these organisms&#x27; varying tolerance to pollutants, their wide distribution in diverse aquatic habitats, and their largely sedentary nature make them ideal for reflecting localized environmental conditions.

The utility of macroinvertebrates in environmental monitoring stems from their role as bioindicators, meaning their presence, absence, or abundance can signal the health of an ecosystem. Their community composition reveals the cumulative impacts of various stressors over time, such as organic pollution or habitat alterations, providing critical insights that often surpass what can be measured through chemical or physical assessments alone. For instance, the presence of pollution-sensitive species, or conversely, the dominance of more tolerant species, can serve as early warning signs of ecological stress, guiding timely intervention and restoration efforts (Rosenberg &amp; Resh, 1993).

The article also underscores the necessity for a comprehensive and multi-faceted approach to environmental monitoring, aligning closely with our course readings that advocate for the integration of biological, chemical, and physical indicators in ecosystem assessments. The use of macroinvertebrates complements traditional water quality measures, offering a more holistic understanding of ecosystem health. This approach not only enhances our ability to detect and quantify environmental changes but also supports more effective management strategies and policy development aimed at conserving freshwater ecosystems.

I chose this article because of its strong foundation in peer-reviewed research, having been published in a highly respected journal within the field of aquatic ecology. Its credibility and scientific rigor make it a valuable source for this discussion, as it is based on well-established ecological studies. The article provides not only a detailed analysis of macroinvertebrate sampling as a method for monitoring freshwater ecosystems but also offers deeper insights into the scientific principles that underpin its use.

The broader significance of macroinvertebrate sampling lies in its role in long-term ecological monitoring. Since these organisms respond to a wide array of environmental stressors, they provide invaluable data on the state of aquatic ecosystems over time. The diversity and abundance of species in a given habitat are key indicators of ecosystem health, contributing essential information for guiding conservation and restoration projects. As Rosenberg and Resh (1993) emphasize, integrating biological monitoring into environmental assessments yields a more accurate and complete picture of water quality, a necessity for sustainable environmental management.

Macroinvertebrate sampling stands as a scientifically validated method central to the assessment of aquatic ecosystem health. The article from Freshwater Science (Rosenberg &amp; Resh, 1993) contributes a rigorous, evidence-based perspective that reinforces the critical importance of biological monitoring in understanding and protecting freshwater ecosystems.

Best regards,

Bob

 

Reference:

Rosenberg, D. M., &amp; Resh, V. H. (1993). The use of benthic macroinvertebrates in the biological monitoring of freshwater ecosystems: The North American experience. Journal of the North American Benthological Society, 12(3), 227-244. https://doi.org/10.2307/1467612

Rodolfo Cortez-Week 3 discussion
Contains unread posts
Rodolfo Cortez posted Sep 18, 2024 11:37 PM
Hello class,

For this week’s discussion I chose to look up a local volunteer water sampling organization. The Texas Stream Team is an all-volunteer organization that works across the state of Texas. The local branch for me is in San Antonio, TX. Volunteers from partner organizations and citizen scientists monitor the water quality and try to obtain data relating to concerns in the collected samples they take from streams, lakes, and rivers. The team is part of a statewide project that work as a collective on an annual basis. Data collected from each team is submitted to a library of samples and used to assess effectiveness of existing best management practices at the affected sites.

I chose to review this project due to it’s close relationship to the work we have been discussing over the last three weeks.  In researching The Texas Stream Team’s website, I found a study they are working on to project the amount of available groundwater available in six counties. The chosen counties are seeing substantial population growth. According to the study, the cumulative population between the six counties is expected to grow by around 3 million more people by 2070. In relation to this course, scientists have predicted that the local aquifer will be utilized by them all at some point and therefore become an area of concern for increased pumping. The study is still in progress, which makes the completed report or any visual content scarce at the moment. However, If you would like to learn more about the project and the Texas Stream Team, you can visit their website. https://www.tceq.texas.gov/waterquality/nonpoint-source/projects/texas-stream-team

References:

(TCEQ)Texas Commission on Environmental Quality (2024, June 18). Texas Stream Team Program: Statewide Volunteer Monitoring. Texas Commission on Environmental Quality. Retrieved September 18, 2024, from https://www.tceq.texas.gov/waterquality/nonpoint-source/projects/texas-stream-team

Texas State University (n.d.). Groundwater Availability Trends. The Meadows Center for Water and the Environment. Retrieved September 18, 2024, from https://www.meadowscenter.txst.edu/research/environmental-flows/groundwater-availability-trends.html

Texas Stream Team
Contains unread posts
Sara Henry posted Sep 19, 2024 1:17 PM
Hi class, 

For this discussion I chose a random state, Texas, to locate and research a water monitoring program. I discovered the Texas Stream Team, a statewide network of trained volunteers and partners. The Meadow Center for Water and the Environment at Texas State University directs the Texas Stream Team (TST). Volunteers and partners gather and analyze data to learn about water quality and non-point source contamination around the state, which they then share with the people of the communities. Volunteers for this initiative are taught to monitor water quality in their local lakes, streams, and estuaries. They then gather data and enter it into a database at the Meadows Center for Water and Environment. The data is then utilized to identify water quality issues and implement the most effective management methods.

There are currently over 400 volunteers that contribute to this initiative. The citizen scientist project was the most fascinating to me, so I looked deeper into the certifications and trainings that are required for it. There are three stages to the training. Phases 1 and 2 are combined for a one-day group training in a classroom environment to learn how to monitor and operate the equipment properly. Once they become confident in using the equipment, the volunteers are then escorted out into the field to demonstrate their expertise. Lastly, Phase 3 is a one-on-one session in which participants do tests at a designated monitoring station that is under very limited observation or supervision. Finally, when the volunteers have completed all three rounds of the training, they will then be certified as H-GAC Texas Stream Water Quality Monitors. 

Sara 

Texas Stream Team Program: Statewide Volunteer Monitoring. Texas Commission on Environmental Quality. (n.d.). https://www.tceq.texas.gov/waterquality/nonpoint-source/projects/texas-stream-team

W3: Macroinvertebrates

Selah Jimenez posted Sep 18, 2024 11:13 PM
Good evening,

Macroinvertebrates are tested because they are indicators of water health as they will not live in water that is unclean. On page 216 of The Science of Water, it says that macroinvertebrates are able to be easily monitored long term and are sensitive to their environments. (Spellman, Frank R, 2014) According to this week’s module, water determines how invertebrates develop based on multiple factors including temperature, dissolved oxygen content, and other minerals in the water. Dissolved oxygen is important because it is a water health indicator and it is the oxygen available to animals that allows them to breathe. Other dissolved gasses impact the health of water regarding pH and alkalinity. This in turn affects the plant life and animals. (Pg 100, Spellman, Frank R, 2014) 


I chose the following study because it was posted by the Environmental Protection Agency and is a reputable source. Macroinvertebrate sampling is used as a representative sample of the habitats to examine the health of the freshwater systems. The report notes that they chose macroinvertebrates due to their little amount of movement and long life spans. Their presence in the freshwater systems represent the health of the streams over long periods of time. For example, if there are changes in the water, the behavior of the macroinvertebrates may change to reflect this. The EPA sampled from all available habitats in the wadable freshwater streams. The report covers safety measures, the process of obtaining samples, and quality control measures. You can read the report here: Multi-Habitat Macroinvertebrate Sampling (epa.gov)

Week 3-Western Pennsylvania Conservancy
Contains unread posts
Sierra Barker posted Sep 18, 2024 8:54 PM
Hi everyone,

The Western Pennsylvania Conservancy’s water quality monitoring program is a volunteer initiative that helps protect local waterways. Volunteers collect data on key water quality factors, like nitrates, phosphates, dissolved oxygen (DO), pH, turbidity( the measure of the relative clarity of a liquid), and temperature. These factors help determine if water is polluted or healthy for life (Western Pennsylvania Conservancy, 2024). Volunteers also study aquatic insects and organisms (bioindicators), which are very sensitive to pollution. The program addresses issues like nutrient pollution from farm runoff, sediment, and erosion. This ties directly to water science, where participants learn about water chemistry, pollution control, and ecosystem health (Western Pennsylvania Conservancy, 2024).

Volunteers gain practical knowledge in watershed management, helping them understand how human activities like farming, land development, and pollution impact local water systems. The program also emphasizes the restoration of riparian zones—the vegetated areas along streams and rivers that act as natural filters, reducing erosion and runoff. The data collected helps guide decisions on how to improve and protect water bodies from harmful pollutants (Western Pennsylvania Conservancy, 2024).

In terms of water sciences, this program provides valuable experience in understanding hydrological cycles (how water moves through the environment), water chemistry (the balance of substances in water), and ecosystem interactions (how living things depend on water). The practical skills learned in the field, like testing water samples and assessing aquatic life, connect directly to scientific concepts taught in water science courses, making the program a bridge between theory and real-world conservation efforts (Western Pennsylvania Conservancy, 2024).

References
Western Pennsylvania Conservancy. (2024, 9 18). Water Quality Monitoring. Retrieved from Western Pennsylvania Conservancy: https://waterlandlife.org/water-quality-monitoring/

Human Sounding Writing Style Guide

Adopt a more human-like writing style and avoid an overly formal or robotic tone. Follow these guidelines to achieve a natural, engaging voice:
Conversational Tone: Write as if having a conversation with a friend. Use a friendly, approachable tone that is relaxed yet respectful. Incorporate contractions (e.g., don&#x27;t, can&#x27;t, it&#x27;s) to sound more natural.
Sentence Variety: Mix short, medium, and long sentences to create a pleasant rhythm. This helps keep the reader engaged and makes the content dynamic.
Personal Touch: Use personal pronouns (I, you, we) to foster a sense of connection. Include anecdotes or relatable examples to illustrate points, making the content more engaging.
Simplicity and Clarity: Use clear, straightforward language. Avoid jargon or overly complex words unless necessary. Break down complex ideas into simpler, more digestible parts.
Empathy and Understanding: Show empathy and acknowledge the reader&#x27;s feelings or concerns. Use supportive phrases like &quot;I understand how you feel&quot; or &quot;That makes sense.&quot;
Humor and Wit: Incorporate light humor or wit where appropriate to make the content enjoyable. Use humor sparingly and ensure it fits the context.
Active Voice: Use active voice to create direct, dynamic sentences. For example, say &quot;I recommend trying this approach&quot; instead of &quot;This approach is recommended.&quot;
Pacing and Flow: Maintain a smooth flow by connecting sentences and paragraphs logically. Use transitional phrases to guide the reader.
Natural Phrasing: Avoid overly formal or technical phrasing. Use language that feels natural and spontaneous.
Smart Brevity: Focus on delivering concise, clear, and impactful messages.
Feedback and Adaptation: Pay attention to feedback and adjust the style based on what resonates most with the reader. Continuously strive to improve the naturalness of the writing.

Style Guide for Beautiful Writing

Hello ChatGPT! I want you to enhance your writing skills based on the following lesson, which emphasizes the importance of sentence variety to create engaging and dynamic content. Please use this as a guide when creating written content for me.

Lesson on effective writing:

This sentence has five words. Here are five more words. Five-word sentences are fine. But several together become monotonous. Listen to what is happening. The writing is getting boring. The sound of it drones. It&#x27;s like a stuck record. The ear demands some variety.

Now listen. I vary the sentence length, and I create music. Music. The writing sings. It has a pleasant rhythm, a lilt, a harmony. I use short sentences. And I use sentences of medium length. And sometimes when I am certain the reader is rested, I will engage him with a sentence of considerable length, a sentence that burns with energy and builds with all the impetus of a crescendo, the roll of the drums, the crash of the cymbals-sounds that say listen to this, it is important.

So write with a combination of short, medium, and long sentences. Create a sound that pleases the reader&#x27;s ear. Don&#x27;t just write words. Write music.

Guidelines:

Vary Sentence Length: Use a mix of short, medium, and long sentences to create a pleasant rhythm in your writing. This variety helps to maintain the reader&#x27;s interest and makes the content more engaging.

Engage the Reader: Use longer, more complex sentences sparingly to emphasize important points and to create a sense of build-up and climax in your writing.

Create Musicality: Aim to create a flow in your writing that feels like music. Think about the rhythm and harmony of your sentences, ensuring they are pleasing to read.

Avoid Monotony: Steer clear of using too many short sentences in a row, as this can become monotonous. Balance is key to maintaining the reader&#x27;s attention.

Emphasize Key Points: Use varied sentence structures to highlight important information and to keep the reader engaged throughout the text.

By following these guidelines, you will be able to create written content that is dynamic, engaging, and enjoyable for readers. Let&#x27;s write content that not only conveys information but also delights the reader&#x27;s senses.

Kelly Orndorff
yesterday at 5:59 PM
Abel,

Higher levels in the food chain, like fish, eat macroinvertebrates as a food source and the macroinvertebrates also aid in the cycling of nutrients. Aquatic ecosystems have different feeding groups of macroinvertebrates like shredders, collectors, grazers and predators (Stumpf et al., 2009). Like you said researching their numbers in helps to aid in the knowledge of growth, development or change within a ecosystem. How do macroinvertebrates modify their habitats, and how does this impact other organisms? Does modifying their habitat show how healthy an aquatic ecosystem is or is not?

Stumpf, S., Valentine-Darby, P., &amp; Gwilliam, E. (2009). Aquatic Macroinvertebrates. National Parks Service. https://www.nps.gov/articles/aquatic-macroinvertebrates-ecological-role.htm

View profile card for Abel Castaneda Abel Castaneda
yesterday at 6:07 PM
Kelly, thank you for the question. Macroinvertebrates modify their habitats through activities like burrowing, feeding, and constructing shelters. For instance, burrowing species help aerate the sediment, promoting oxygen exchange and nutrient cycling, which supports plant growth and provides food for other organisms. Filter-feeders, such as certain insect larvae, help clean the water by removing particles and excess nutrients. These modifications can positively impact other organisms by creating more habitable conditions for fish, plants, and smaller invertebrates. The way macroinvertebrates modify their habitat can indeed indicate ecosystem health. In healthy ecosystems, there&#x27;s a diversity of macroinvertebrate species performing various ecological roles, which maintains balance. Conversely, in degraded ecosystems, pollution-tolerant species dominate, reducing habitat complexity and biodiversity, signaling ecological stress (Stumpf et al., 2009). Thank you for providing the source!

Reference: Stumpf, S., Valentine-Darby, P., &amp; Gwilliam, E. (2009). Aquatic macroinvertebrates: Ecological role. National Parks Service. Retrieved from https://www.nps.gov/articles/aquatic-macroinvertebrates-ecological-role.htm

**ASSISTANT**:
Here are the answers to your quiz based on the provided questions:

1. **True** – Warm water holds less dissolved oxygen than cold water, as temperature affects the solubility of gases like oxygen in water.

2. **True** – Living organisms do require trace amounts of certain heavy metals, but excessive levels of others like cadmium, lead, and mercury can be harmful.

3. **Inorganic** – Matter that does not contain carbon and is not derived from living matter is referred to as inorganic.

4. **Matching:**
   - **Volatile organic compounds (VOCs):** 2. Dangerous type of organic chemical; routes of exposure include inhalation and dermal absorption; can occur in any tap water source regardless of source water or location.
   - **Fluorides:** 3. Controversial water additive; toxic to humans in large quantities.
   - **Synthetic organic chemicals (SOCs):** 1. Manmade; &gt;50,000 in commercial production; examples include dioxin.

5. **Acidic** – A solution with a pH of 2.0 is considered acidic.

6. **True** – The rate of adsorption and desorption of metals to and from sediment is controlled by water chemistry.

7. **True** – When two or more substances are completely soluble in each other, they are considered miscible.

8. **Matching:**
   - **Cl:** 1. Chlorine
   - **Ca:** 8. Calcium
   - **F:** 5. Fluorine
   - **Cu:** 2. Copper
   - **C:** 6. Carbon
   - **Fe:** 4. Iron
   - **He:** 7. Helium
   - **H:** 3. Hydrogen

9. **True** – Parts per million (ppm) are interchangeable with milligrams per liter (mg/L) for mass concentration measurements.

10. **Alkalinity** – Alkalinity is the measure of water&#x27;s ability to neutralize acid and act as a buffer.

11. **pH** – pH measures the hydrogen ion concentration in a solution, indicating whether it&#x27;s acidic or basic.

12. **True** – Synthetic organic compounds (SOCs) are manmade and can be toxic because they do not naturally occur in the environment.

13. **Matching:**
   - **Solvent:** 2. The component that does the dissolving.
   - **Solute:** 3. The component that is dissolved.
   - **Solution:** 1. A condition in which one or more substances are uniformly and evenly mixed or dissolved.

14. **False** – Nitrogen in drinking water can have significant health impacts, such as causing methemoglobinemia or &quot;blue baby syndrome.&quot;

15. **True** – In urban areas, factors like temperature, nitrate, phosphorus, and dissolved oxygen demand increase with higher density.

16. **Organic compounds** – These include substances like carbohydrates, cotton, dyes, fats, and fuels.

17. **Matching:**
   - **Molecule:** 2. The smallest particle of matter or compound that possesses the same composition and characteristics as the rest of the substance.
   - **Ion:** 6. An atom or group of atoms that carries a positive or negative electric charge as a result of having lost or gained one or more electrons.
   - **Atom:** 1. The smallest particle of matter that can unite chemically with other elements.
   - **Element:** 5. The simplest form of chemical matter.
   - **Cation:** 4. A positively charged ion.
   - **Anion:** 3. A negatively charged ion.

18. **Chemical coagulation removes:**
   - **Iron and manganese**
   - **Tastes and odor**
   - **Turbidity and color**
   - **Bacteria and organic pollutants**

19. **Human health hazards related to central nervous system disorders:**
   - **Mercury**
   - **Selenium**

20. **False** – Soft water has 0-75 mg/L CaCO3, but very hard water has &gt;150 mg/L CaCO3 (not &gt;75 mg/L).

Let me know if you need further clarification on any of the answers!

**ASSISTANT**:
It looks like none of the listed waterborne organisms are directly related to *Staphylococcus* infections, which are typically caused by *Staphylococcus aureus* and aren&#x27;t typically waterborne. However, if you would like to incorporate a connection to your daughter&#x27;s experience, you could focus on public health, highlighting how infections—whether waterborne like *E. coli* or bacteria like *Staphylococcus*—pose threats to vulnerable populations. This would keep the tone personal and relevant.

Here’s a structure for your assignment with a focus on *Escherichia coli* (E. coli), a bacterium that could align well with your interest in health and waterborne diseases. You can build your narration around these talking points:

---

**Name: George Steward**  
**Date: September 23, 2024**

---

### 1. Selected Waterborne Concern:
I selected *Escherichia coli* (E. coli), a coliform bacterium often found in the intestines of humans and animals. While most strains of *E. coli* are harmless, some—like the Shiga toxin-producing *E. coli* (STEC)—can cause severe foodborne and waterborne illnesses.

---

### 2. Primary Routes of Exposure:
*E. coli* enters the human body primarily through ingestion of contaminated food or water. Exposure can occur when people drink untreated water, eat undercooked meat, or consume raw vegetables irrigated with contaminated water. Swimming in contaminated water bodies can also result in infection if water is accidentally swallowed.

---

### 3. Major Human Health Impacts:
*E. coli* infections can lead to a range of health impacts, from mild gastrointestinal distress to life-threatening conditions like hemolytic uremic syndrome (HUS). The CDC estimates around 265,000 STEC infections occur annually in the United States, with serious cases requiring hospitalization . Outbreaks of *E. coli* have been reported across the globe, including significant cases in the U.S., often linked to contaminated food or water sources. These infections generally have short-term impacts, such as diarrhea, abdominal cramps, and vomiting, but severe cases like HUS can lead to kidney failure, particularly in young children.

---

### 4. Life Cycle of *E. coli*:
The life cycle of *E. coli* begins when it is introduced into the environment, typically through fecal matter. Once inside a host, *E. coli* attaches to the intestinal wall, where it multiplies rapidly. For pathogenic strains like STEC, this is when the bacterium begins producing toxins that cause illness. The organism reproduces through binary fission, a simple form of asexual reproduction, which allows it to spread rapidly within a contaminated environment or host. The bacteria can then be shed in the feces of infected individuals or animals, further contaminating water sources.

---

### 5. Diagnosis and Treatment:
Diagnosis of *E. coli* infections is typically done by stool culture, which helps identify the presence of harmful strains like STEC. In terms of treatment, most cases resolve on their own with rest and hydration, but severe infections may require hospitalization, especially in cases of dehydration or kidney complications like HUS. Antibiotics are usually not recommended for treating STEC infections as they may increase toxin production【7†source】.

---

### 6. Removal in Drinking Water Treatment:
In conventional drinking water treatment facilities, *E. coli* is removed through multiple stages of treatment. The process typically includes coagulation, filtration, and disinfection (usually chlorination or ultraviolet light treatment). Chlorine and UV effectively kill *E. coli* bacteria, ensuring that the treated water is safe for consumption .

---

### References (APA Style):
Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. (2023). E. coli (Escherichia coli). https://www.cdc.gov/ecoli/index.html

World Health Organization. (2023). E. coli. https://www.who.int/news-room/fact-sheets/detail/e-coli

Siwak, A. M., Baker, P. G., &amp; Dube, A. (2023). Biosensors as early warning detection systems for waterborne Cryptosporidium. Water Science &amp; Technology, 88(3), 615–626. https://doi.org/10.2166/wst.2023.229 

Troeger, C., Khalil, I. A., Rao, P. C., Blacker, B. F., Brown, A., Brewer, T. G., ... &amp; Reiner, R. C. (2017). Cryptosporidium-related morbidity and mortality among children under five. Lancet Global Health, 6(7), e758-e768. https://doi.org/10.1016/S2214-109X(18)30283-3

**Audio Script:**

&quot;Hi, I’m George, and today I’m going to talk about *Escherichia coli*, or *E. coli*. Now, we often hear about *E. coli* in the context of infections and contaminated water, but did you know that *E. coli* is actually a major player in scientific research and development?

Most strains of *E. coli* are harmless and naturally live in the intestines of humans and animals. However, in research labs, this humble bacterium is a workhorse, helping scientists produce all kinds of useful compounds. One of the coolest things I’ve come across recently is how *E. coli* is being used to produce substances like psilocybin, which is the active compound in magic mushrooms. Researchers at the University of Florida have been experimenting with genetically modified *E. coli* to produce psilocybin in the lab. This is groundbreaking because it could lead to more efficient and scalable production of psilocybin, which is being studied for its potential mental health benefits, especially in treating depression and anxiety.

What makes *E. coli* so great for this kind of work is its ability to grow quickly and its well-understood genetic structure. Scientists can easily insert genes from other organisms into *E. coli*, and it will start producing the proteins or compounds associated with those genes. It’s like turning *E. coli* into tiny biological factories.

Besides psilocybin, *E. coli* has been used to produce insulin for diabetes treatment since the 1980s. The ability to create life-saving drugs at a lower cost and on a large scale is one of the reasons *E. coli* remains at the forefront of biotech research. Scientists have even engineered *E. coli* to create biofuels, making this bacterium not just a part of healthcare solutions but also potentially part of our future energy needs.

Now, don’t get me wrong, *E. coli* can still be dangerous in the wrong context, especially strains like STEC, which cause foodborne illnesses. But overall, it’s important to see the bigger picture. In water systems, it serves as an indicator of fecal contamination, helping us monitor the safety of drinking water. And in the lab, it’s a key ally in advancing medicine and science.

So, while *E. coli* may have a bad reputation in some areas, it’s also one of the most useful tools we have in biotechnology. It’s a fascinating example of how something that can be harmful in one context can be incredibly beneficial in another.

Thanks for listening.&quot;

**Script for Waterborne Pathogen Presentation - *Escherichia coli***:

&quot;Hi, I’m George, and today I’ll be talking about *Escherichia coli*, commonly known as *E. coli*. While *E. coli* often gets a bad rap due to its association with foodborne illnesses and water contamination, it’s not all negative. In fact, *E. coli* has some pretty incredible applications in science and research.

Let’s start with the basics. *E. coli* is a coliform bacterium, which means it’s found in the intestines of humans and animals. It’s also one of the primary organisms we monitor in water systems because it indicates fecal contamination. *E. coli* enters the body primarily through ingestion of contaminated food or water, leading to gastrointestinal issues. However, not all strains are harmful. The harmful strains, like *E. coli* O157:H7, cause severe illness, but other strains are actually harmless and incredibly useful in research.

The health impacts of *E. coli* infections range from mild discomfort to more severe symptoms like bloody diarrhea, kidney failure, or even death. In the U.S., *E. coli* O157:H7 causes around 73,000 cases of illness each year, and outbreaks tend to happen where food or water sources become contaminated with fecal matter. One well-known outbreak occurred in 1993 when *E. coli* contaminated undercooked hamburgers, leading to stricter food safety regulations.

Now, when it comes to research, *E. coli* is a star. Scientists have harnessed its fast growth and simple genetic makeup to produce all sorts of valuable substances. For instance, at the University of Florida, researchers are using genetically modified *E. coli* to produce psilocybin, the active compound found in magic mushrooms. This is particularly exciting because psilocybin has shown potential in treating mental health conditions like depression. And it’s not just psilocybin; *E. coli* has been used to produce insulin for diabetes treatment since the 1980s. Its role in biotechnology is indispensable.

As for how *E. coli* is treated and diagnosed in humans, healthcare providers typically use stool tests to confirm the presence of harmful strains. Treatments vary depending on the severity of the infection, but they often involve rest, fluids, and, in some cases, antibiotics. The key is early detection, which brings us back to why monitoring water for *E. coli* is so important.

When it comes to removing *E. coli* from drinking water, conventional treatment methods like filtration, sedimentation, and disinfection with chlorine or UV light are typically effective. These processes work to either kill the bacteria or physically remove them from the water supply.

In summary, while *E. coli* is often associated with health risks, especially in waterborne contamination, it also plays a vital role in scientific advancements, contributing to the development of life-saving treatments and research innovations.&quot;

---

---

**Script for Waterborne Pathogen Presentation - *Escherichia coli* (E. coli)**

&quot;Hi, I’m George, and today I’ll be talking about *Escherichia coli*, or *E. coli*. While *E. coli* often gets a lot of attention due to its association with foodborne illnesses and water contamination, it’s important to recognize that it’s not always a bad actor. In fact, *E. coli* plays a pretty important role in scientific research and development.

Let’s first look at the basics. *E. coli* is a bacterium that lives in the intestines of humans and animals and can enter water sources through fecal contamination. We usually hear about it in relation to water testing, as its presence is a key indicator of contamination. *E. coli* gets into our bodies mainly through the ingestion of contaminated food or water, which can lead to gastrointestinal issues. But not all strains are harmful. For instance, strains like *E. coli* O157:H7 are dangerous, causing about 73,000 illnesses in the U.S. each year (Centers for Disease Control and Prevention [CDC], 2023). The severity can range from stomach cramps and diarrhea to life-threatening conditions like kidney failure (Mayo Clinic, 2023). 

Interestingly though, *E. coli* isn’t just a villain. It’s also a hero in research labs. Scientists have been using *E. coli* for decades to produce substances like insulin and even more recently, psilocybin, the active compound in magic mushrooms that shows promise in treating depression (University of Florida, 2022). In these labs, researchers can modify *E. coli* to produce valuable chemicals, making it an essential tool in biotechnology. This side of *E. coli* highlights its versatility, proving that it’s not just about waterborne diseases.

As for diagnosis and treatment of *E. coli* infections, healthcare professionals often use stool tests to identify harmful strains. Most infections are treated with rest and hydration, although antibiotics are sometimes necessary in severe cases (CDC, 2023). And when it comes to water, conventional treatment methods like filtration, sedimentation, and chlorination are effective at removing *E. coli* from drinking water (U.S. Environmental Protection Agency [EPA], 2020). These processes either kill the bacteria or filter them out, ensuring water is safe for human consumption.

In summary, *E. coli* may have a negative reputation, especially when it contaminates water, but its contributions to research and medicine cannot be overstated. It’s a reminder that context matters, and with proper management and scientific application, this bacterium can be incredibly beneficial.&quot;

---

**Graph Recommendation**:
To enhance your presentation visually, include a graph showing the effectiveness of **different filtration methods** (e.g., sand filtration, reverse osmosis) in removing *E. coli* from contaminated water. The EPA provides excellent data on water filtration methods and their effectiveness in reducing bacterial concentrations.

You can explore **EPA’s Safe Drinking Water Information** on filtration efficiency [here](https://www.epa.gov/ground-water-and-drinking-water) or use public health and scientific research data to create a graph showcasing the **reduction in *E. coli* contamination** after different stages of water treatment (EPA, 2020).

---

**References (APA format)**:
Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. (2023). *E. coli* (Escherichia coli). Retrieved from https://www.cdc.gov/ecoli/general/index.html

Mayo Clinic. (2023). *E. coli* infection. Retrieved from https://www.mayoclinic.org/diseases-conditions/e-coli/symptoms-causes/syc-20372058

University of Florida. (2022). UF researchers engineer *E. coli* to produce psilocybin. Retrieved from https://research.ufl.edu/2022/09/psilocybin.html

U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2020). Drinking water treatment. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/ground-water-and-drinking-water

**Speaking the References in Audio**:
When recording the audio, you don’t need to speak the full citations like in the written document. Instead, you can mention the source casually or say, “According to the CDC” or “A study by the University of Florida.” At the end of the audio, you can say, &quot;For detailed references, please see the written document.&quot;

This way, your script is concise, natural, and informative, and you can handle the references appropriately in both the written and audio formats. Let me know if you&#x27;d like to make any adjustments!

I&#x27;ll provide a detailed guide on how you can recreate this chart using Excel or Google Sheets:

### Steps to Create the Chart:

1. **Open Excel or Google Sheets**:
   - In the first column, list the filtration methods: &quot;Reverse Osmosis,&quot; &quot;UV Light,&quot; &quot;Activated Carbon,&quot; &quot;One Micron Filtration,&quot; &quot;Distillation.&quot;
   - In the second column, add the corresponding effectiveness values: 99.9, 95, 85, 70, and 99.9.

2. **Create a Bar Chart**:
   - Highlight both columns.
   - In **Excel**: Go to **Insert** → **Bar Chart** and select a **2D Bar Chart**.
   - In **Google Sheets**: Go to **Insert** → **Chart**, and under **Chart Type**, select a **Bar Chart**.

3. **Customize the Chart**:
   - Add a title: &quot;Effectiveness of Filtration Methods in Removing E. coli from Water.&quot;
   - Label the X-axis as **Filtration Methods** and the Y-axis as **Effectiveness (%)**.
   - Set the Y-axis scale to range between 60% and 100% for better visualization.

### Chart Information:
- **Methods**: The various filtration methods listed in the chart.
- **Effectiveness**: The percentage values represent the effectiveness of each filtration method in removing **E. coli**.

### Citation for the EPA Document on Filtration:
You can include the following citation in your reference list based on the EPA document:

**Reference**:  
U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2001). Filtration and Disinfection to Remove E. coli. *EPA 816-F-01-027*. Retrieved from [https://www.epa.gov](https://www.epa.gov)

**ASSISTANT**:
Here are the answers based on the understanding of water science principles:

**Question 1:**
Microorganisms of interest to water practitioners and students of the science of water include which of the following?  
*Answer:*
- Fungi
- Rotifers
- Bacteria
- Viruses
- Algae
- Protozoa

**Question 2:**
Match each term with the best trophic level description.  
*Answer:*
1. First trophic level → **Producers; includes green plants that use sunlight to convert inorganic compounds into organic compounds, which are used as a food source by animals**
2. Decomposers → **Feed on dead plants or animals; important/critical role in nutrient recycling; bacteria and fungi are examples**
3. Third trophic level → **Secondary consumers; eat primary consumers (e.g. fish)**
4. Second trophic level → **Primary consumers; eat plants directly; herbivores (e.g. zooplankton)**
5. Fourth trophic level (top level) → **Tertiary consumers; eat other lower trophic level consumers**

**Question 3:**
____________ is the process of enrichment of an environment with inorganic substances (e.g., nitrogen, phosphorus) that causes excessive algae growth and aging of an aquatic body.  
*Answer:*  
Eutrophication

**Question 4:**
Which of the following terms describe feeding groups (or trophic groups) of macroinvertebrates found in streams?  
*Answer:*
- Collectors
- Scrapers
- Predators
- Shredders

**Question 5:**
Select the best answer for each response.  
*Answer:*
1. Flood plain → **The self-formed, self-adjusted flat area near the stream is the __________, which loosely describes the valley floor prone to periodic inundation during overbank discharges.**
2. Abiotic factor → **The nonliving part of the environment composed of sunlight, soil, mineral elements, moisture, temperature, and topography.**
3. Secondary succession → **An observed process of change that occurs (as a normal occurrence in nature) in the species structure of an ecological community over time in an area where the natural vegetation has been removed or destroyed but the soil is not destroyed.**

**Question 6:**
The ___________, implemented in 1989 by the ____________, addresses waterborne diseases by requiring both filtration and disinfection of all surface water supplies as a means of primarily controlling Giardia and enteric viruses. In 1996, regulations also included Cryptosporidium.  
*Answer:*  
Surface Water Treatment Rule; US Environmental Protection Agency (EPA)

**Question 7:**
Which of the following is NOT a modern tenet of cell theory?  
*Answer:*  
None of the above. All answers are modern tenets of cell theory.

**Question 8:**
All organisms that share the same general type of food in a food chain are said to be at the same _______ (nourishment or feeding level).  
*Answer:*  
Trophic level

**Question 9:**
Match the term with the best definition.  
*Answer:*  
1. Algae → **Simple plants; diversity of shapes, sizes, color, and habitats; convert inorganic constituents in water into organic constituents**
2. Prokaryotic cell → **Simple cell type; lacks nuclear membrane; lacks mitochondria**
3. Fungi → **Simple plant; lacks ability to produce energy through photosynthesis**

**Question 10:**
There are three types of ecological pyramids. Select the answer that is NOT one of the three recognized types.  
*Answer:*  
Pyramids of size

**Question 11:**
The ultimate carrying capacity is the theoretical maximum density; that is, it is the maximum number of individuals of a species in a place that can support itself without rendering the place uninhabitable.  
*Answer:*  
True

**Question 12:**
Net community productivity is described as the rate of storage of organic matter not used.  
*Answer:*  
True

**Question 13:**
Biochemical oxygen demand (BOD) is a widely used parameter of organic pollution often applied to wastewater but rarely to surface water. BOD is the amount of oxygen required by bacteria to stabilize decomposable organic matter under aerobic conditions.  
*Answer:*  
True

**Question 14:**
Giardia has an incubation period of 1 to 8 months.  
*Answer:*  
False

**Question 15:**
One quick and inexpensive method to measure turbidity in the field is by using a transparency tube.  
*Answer:*  
True

**Question 16:**
One of the principles of using the biotic index states that as the degree of pollution in a stream decreases, key organisms tend to disappear in a predictable order. The disappearance of particular organisms tends to represent the water quality of the stream.  
*Answer:*  
True

**Question 17:**
When collecting water samples with a collecting device, rinse the sampler with distilled water to limit cross-contamination, then rinse the sampler three times with sample water to ensure a representative sample.  
*Answer:*  
True

**Question 18:**
Match the stream habitat type and sampling strategies to the best description.  
*Answer:*
1. Snags → **Submerged woody debris that has been in place for a relatively long period of time; sampling for macroinvertebrates includes dislodging organisms from the wood materials.**
2. Cobble (hard substrate) → **Prevalent in riffles and runs; common features in most mountain and piedmont streams; not common in coastal or other low-gradient streams.**
3. Sand (and other fine sediment) → **Generally least productive habitat type; sample for macroinvertebrates by bumping the net along the substrate surface; reduce debris collection by bumping not dragging through the soft substrate.**
4. Submerged macrophytes → **Not common in high gradient streams; seasonal in occurrence; avoid sediments, but jab or bump the rooted vegetation to dislodge organisms.**
5. Vegetated banks → **Submerged lower banks that have roots and emergent plant cover; habitat can be jabbed or kicked to dislodge organisms.**

**Question 19:**
Match each term with the correct definition.  
*Answer:*
1. Habitat assessment → **The use of physical characteristics to assess an aquatic ecosystem.**
2. Biotic index → **A systematic survey of organisms (e.g. benthic macroinvertebrates).**
3. Biomonitoring → **The use of living organisms to assess an aquatic ecosystem.**

**ASSISTANT**:
Let&#x27;s review potential areas where the answers might have gone wrong:

1. **Question 14 (Giardia incubation period):**
   - The answer was marked as **False**, which is correct because Giardia&#x27;s incubation period is typically between 1 to 3 weeks, not months.

2. **Question 13 (BOD definition):**
   - BOD is widely used for wastewater as well as for surface water, so this answer could have caused confusion. However, stating that it&#x27;s &quot;rarely applied to surface water&quot; might have been the error. The correct statement is that **BOD is a standard parameter for measuring organic pollution in both wastewater and surface water.** So, this should be **True.**

3. **Question 6 (Surface Water Treatment Rule):**
   - The answer provided was **Surface Water Treatment Rule; US Environmental Protection Agency (EPA)**, which is correct, so no issue here.

It seems the error is more likely related to BOD and how frequently it&#x27;s applied. To correct this:

- **Question 13**: BOD should be marked as **True**, acknowledging its application to both wastewater and surface water.

Let me know if you&#x27;d like to dive deeper into the topics or clarify further!

Water, Wetlands, and Watersheds Seminar

Join the YouTube livestream Wednesday, October 2nd, 2024 at 11:45am ET for the next W3 seminar of the semester!

Royal C. Gardner, Professor of Law and Director of the Institute for Biodiversity Law and Policy at Stetson University College of Law, will present Unnavigable waters: SCOTUS scuttles WOTUS. You can catch the livestream at https://youtube.com/live/EpJC43eOOYk?feature=share.

The full seminar series schedule is on our website, and our YouTube channel has past recordings and also displays livestreams if there are any issues with the direct link. 


Livestream, October 2nd, 11:45 AM
Our channel (recordings + livestream backup)
Abstract

In May 2023, the U.S. Supreme Court once again waded into the wetland wars. In Sackett v. U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, in a decision written by Justice Samuel Alito, the Court very narrowly interpreted the statutory term “waters of the United States,” also known as WOTUS. If a wetland is a WOTUS, then it is protected by the Clean Water Act. If it is not a WOTUS, then the Clean Water Act does not apply. Brenda Mallory, the Chair of the White House Council on Environmental Quality, called Sackett “one of the largest judicial rollbacks of environmental protections in U.S. history.” The first thing that must be done, she said, is “to be honest, clear, vocal, and visual about the impact of the Supreme Court’s ruling.” This presentation provides such an assessment.

Bio: Royal C. Gardner is Professor of Law and Director of the Institute for Biodiversity Law and Policy at Stetson University College of Law. He has worked on wetland law and policy issues for more than three decades, beginning, oddly enough, at the Pentagon. Recent projects include serving as the Chair of the Scientific and Technical Review Panel of the Ramsar Convention on Wetlands, testifying before a World Bank arbitration panel, and coauthoring amici briefs on behalf of environmental scientists and scientific societies. He was lead counsel for an amici brief filed on behalf of twelve national and international scientific societies in Sackett v. U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, as well as for the amici brief filed for aquatic scientists and scientific societies that the U.S. Supreme Court cited in the case of County of Maui v. Hawaii Wildlife Fund. He was a lead coordinating co-author of the Global Wetlands Outlook, the Ramsar Convention’s flagship publication on the state of the world’s wetlands. He is also the author of Lawyers, Swamps, and Money: U.S. Wetland Law, Policy, and Politics, which has a small cult following. His book Waters of the United States: POTUS, SCOTUS, WOTUS, and the Politics of a National Resource will be published by Island Press in December 2024.

You can find the seminar schedule and recordings of past seminars on our website: cfw.essie.ufl.edu/seminars/

*&quot; Substantive&quot; means comments contributing something new and important to the discussion. Thus, a message that simply says &quot;I agree&quot; is not substantive. Instead, a best practice reply:

contributes a new idea or perspective, 

asks good follow-up questions to points made after providing some additional research on the topic,

provides examples or illustrations of a key point,

points out inconsistencies in an argument, and/or

shares a new and relevant resource to further the discussion.

**ASSISTANT**:
**Week 5 Discussion Post: Tackling Water Pollution and Water Treatment**

Recently, I conducted a water test on my tap water and the results were concerning. Living in Georgetown, TX, in the Edwards Plateau region, I’ve always known that we deal with hard water due to the high levels of calcium carbonate from the karst limestone. What I didn’t expect to see was the presence of pesticides in our water. This has raised serious concerns for me, especially when I consider my wife and small children, who already have sensitive skin conditions. Could this exposure be exacerbating their health issues?

We rely on a basic refrigerator water filter, which helps with drinking water and ice production, but that’s not enough to address the whole-house issue. The pesticides detected were a real wake-up call. For a family that takes pride in eating organic and avoiding harmful chemicals, finding out that pesticides are present in our water is disheartening. 

I’ve started researching whole-home filtration systems, which range from $2,000 to $10,000, to ensure that all of the water we use—whether for drinking, bathing, or cooking—is free from contaminants. I’m also planning a full analysis to determine which pesticides are present and in what quantities. Water pollution in our region isn’t just a distant issue—it’s at our doorstep, affecting us every day.

Fortunately, there are solutions to these problems. One particularly promising approach is demonstrated by companies working on advanced water filtration systems that go beyond traditional municipal treatments. For example, some cutting-edge whole-home filtration systems now utilize activated carbon filters combined with reverse osmosis, which is highly effective at removing not just hard minerals but also harmful contaminants like pesticides, volatile organic compounds (VOCs), and other pollutants. In areas like mine, where hard water and agricultural runoff intersect, this type of system could be a game changer for families seeking to improve their water quality.

While this journey has been overwhelming, I’m hopeful that advancements in water treatment can offer peace of mind. It’s frustrating to have to invest in additional protection on top of already paying for municipal water, but the health of my family is non-negotiable.

References:
- Georgetown Utilities. (2024). &quot;Water Quality Report for Georgetown, TX.&quot; Retrieved from [Georgetown Water Quality](https://www.georgetownwater.org)
- Environmental Protection Agency. (2020). &quot;Understanding Pesticides in Drinking Water.&quot; Retrieved from [EPA Website](https://www.epa.gov)
- Filtration Systems, Inc. (2022). &quot;Whole-Home Filtration Solutions: A Guide.&quot;

**ASSISTANT**:
**Week 5 Discussion Post: Addressing Water Pollution and Treatment Solutions through Soil Regeneration**

Recently, I conducted a water test on my tap water, and the results were alarming. Living in the Edwards Plateau region of Georgetown, TX, we’ve always known that our water is hard due to the high levels of calcium carbonate from the limestone. However, the most concerning revelation was the presence of pesticides in our water. This hits especially close to home for me and my family, as we not only eat organic food but also grow organic produce and raise our animals as naturally as possible. To find out that these harmful chemicals have infiltrated our water supply is both disturbing and disheartening.

While we use a basic refrigerator water filter, it only addresses our drinking water. We are now researching whole-home filtration systems to ensure that the water we use for everything—drinking, bathing, cooking—is clean and free from contaminants. These systems, ranging from $2,000 to $10,000, could be a much-needed solution to safeguard our health. However, this situation has made me reflect on the root of the problem: pesticide runoff.

One of the key ways we can tackle pesticide runoff is by focusing on soil health, which is at the core of my work through my company. Healthy soil is the foundation for minimizing chemical runoff into water supplies. I have been working with biostimulants, which accelerate soil regeneration by improving microbial activity and overall soil structure. This promotes natural fertility, reducing the need for synthetic fertilizers and chemicals that contribute to water contamination.

Another effective solution is the use of biochar. Biochar is a type of charcoal that, when added to soil, helps lock in nutrients, improving water retention and keeping fertilizers and chemicals from running off into water systems. Biochar not only enhances the soil&#x27;s ability to support plant growth, but it also acts as a filter that traps harmful chemicals and prevents them from leaching into nearby water bodies.

By combining these two solutions—biostimulants and biochar—we can work toward regenerating the soil and reducing the reliance on harmful pesticides. This holistic approach not only helps protect water quality but also builds a more resilient ecosystem that supports long-term sustainability.

In a world where the burden of maintaining clean water seems increasingly difficult, focusing on the health of our soil offers a powerful and natural solution. If we invest in soil regeneration, we can significantly reduce water pollution from pesticide runoff, improving both environmental and human health. 

**References**

Environmental Protection Agency. (2020). Understanding pesticides in drinking water. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/pesticides/understanding-pesticides-drinking-water

Georgetown Utilities. (2024). Water quality report for Georgetown, TX. Retrieved from https://georgetownwater.org

Filtration Systems, Inc. (2022). Whole-home filtration solutions: A guide. Retrieved from https://www.filtrationsystems.com/whole-home-solutions

**USER**:
explain how heat is a water pollutant. i need to make a 100% so please double check to make sure we are at that level

---

**ASSISTANT**:
Heat is considered a water pollutant through a process known as **thermal pollution**. This occurs when industries, particularly power plants and manufacturing facilities, discharge heated water back into natural bodies of water such as rivers, lakes, or oceans. The heat alters the water temperature beyond its natural range, which can disrupt local ecosystems in several key ways:

1. **Reduced Oxygen Levels:** Warm water holds less dissolved oxygen than cooler water. This can lead to oxygen depletion, which is critical for the survival of aquatic life, particularly species that rely on higher oxygen levels like fish and macroinvertebrates.

2. **Stress on Aquatic Life:** Sudden or sustained increases in water temperature can stress or even kill aquatic organisms. Many species have a narrow range of temperature tolerance, and exceeding this range can interfere with their metabolism, reproduction, and immune systems.

3. **Ecosystem Disruption:** Warmer waters can cause the migration or displacement of sensitive species and lead to an imbalance in the ecosystem. Invasive species that thrive in warmer temperatures may outcompete native species, further destabilizing the ecosystem.

4. **Harmful Algal Blooms:** Elevated water temperatures can accelerate the growth of harmful algae, which can create *algal blooms*. These blooms often lead to oxygen depletion when the algae die and decompose, and some species of algae can release toxins harmful to both aquatic life and humans.

So, even though heat itself is not a &quot;chemical&quot; pollutant, it alters water chemistry and biology in ways that can severely impact aquatic environments. **Thermal pollution** can have long-lasting negative effects on water quality and biodiversity.

Interested in the Clean Water Act? Check out this livestream on the 2023 US Supreme Court case - Unnavigable waters: SCOTUS scuttles WOTUS

Kelly Reiss posted Oct 1, 2024 9:56 AMThis thread is pinned
Water, Wetlands, and Watersheds Seminar


Join the YouTube livestream Wednesday, October 2nd, 2024 at 11:45am ET for the next W3 seminar of the semester!

Royal C. Gardner, Professor of Law and Director of the Institute for Biodiversity Law and Policy at Stetson University College of Law, will present Unnavigable waters: SCOTUS scuttles WOTUS. You can catch the livestream at https://youtube.com/live/EpJC43eOOYk?feature=share.

The full seminar series schedule is on our website, and our YouTube channel has past recordings and also displays livestreams if there are any issues with the direct link. 


Livestream, October 2nd, 11:45 AM
Our channel (recordings + livestream backup)
Abstract

In May 2023, the U.S. Supreme Court once again waded into the wetland wars. In Sackett v. U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, in a decision written by Justice Samuel Alito, the Court very narrowly interpreted the statutory term “waters of the United States,” also known as WOTUS. If a wetland is a WOTUS, then it is protected by the Clean Water Act. If it is not a WOTUS, then the Clean Water Act does not apply. Brenda Mallory, the Chair of the White House Council on Environmental Quality, called Sackett “one of the largest judicial rollbacks of environmental protections in U.S. history.” The first thing that must be done, she said, is “to be honest, clear, vocal, and visual about the impact of the Supreme Court’s ruling.” This presentation provides such an assessment.

Bio: Royal C. Gardner is Professor of Law and Director of the Institute for Biodiversity Law and Policy at Stetson University College of Law. He has worked on wetland law and policy issues for more than three decades, beginning, oddly enough, at the Pentagon. Recent projects include serving as the Chair of the Scientific and Technical Review Panel of the Ramsar Convention on Wetlands, testifying before a World Bank arbitration panel, and coauthoring amici briefs on behalf of environmental scientists and scientific societies. He was lead counsel for an amici brief filed on behalf of twelve national and international scientific societies in Sackett v. U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, as well as for the amici brief filed for aquatic scientists and scientific societies that the U.S. Supreme Court cited in the case of County of Maui v. Hawaii Wildlife Fund. He was a lead coordinating co-author of the Global Wetlands Outlook, the Ramsar Convention’s flagship publication on the state of the world’s wetlands. He is also the author of Lawyers, Swamps, and Money: U.S. Wetland Law, Policy, and Politics, which has a small cult following. His book Waters of the United States: POTUS, SCOTUS, WOTUS, and the Politics of a National Resource will be published by Island Press in December 2024.

You can find the seminar schedule and recordings of past seminars on our website: cfw.essie.ufl.edu/seminars/

If you are on social media, please consider following our accounts:

facebook.com/ufWetlands/
https://www.linkedin.com/company/uf-howard-t-odum-center-for-wetlands/
twitter.com/ufWetland
youtube.com/c/UFHowardTOdumCenterforWetlands

Review Discussion Week 3
Contains unread posts
Kelly Reiss posted Sep 24, 2024 10:09 AMThis thread is pinned
In the past couple of weeks, we have covered a range of topics, from the water cycle to water hydraulics to water chemistry and water biology. I realize the readings may be a bit technical in some early readings, but they lay a foundation for our future readings on water ecology, pollution, treatment, and even monitoring and assessment. The Clean Water Act directs us as a nation to protect the chemical, physical, and biological integrity of our aquatic bodies (note that integrity is generally considered to be represented by the conditions of unimpacted, pristine systems).

There were so many important points and definitions from the past couple of weeks that it is hard to select only a few to cover in the discussion review. One point I did want to make …. especially for those of you who have already had EVSP311 Soil Science… is that materials are moving around the planet in solutions and suspensions. Water plays a critical role in the mass cycling of all sorts of chemicals on the surface, in the atmosphere, and underground. Have you ever thought about how you personally observe the hydrologic cycle each day beyond simple precipitation events?

I encourage you to take the time to review the discussion that Spellman (2015) presents on the elements, especially some of the more common elements relevant to water science: chlorine (Cl), copper (Cu), hydrogen (H), iron (Fe), nitrogen (N), oxygen (O), phosphorus (P), sodium (Na), and sulfur (S). You probably remember many of those from your introductory biology and chemistry courses… recall the macronutrients and CHNOPS (carbon, hydrogen, nitrogen, oxygen, phosphorus, and sulfur)? In particular, review the textbook sections on water constituents and common water measurements.

Spellman (2015) covers microbiology, reminding us of the important terminology and moving into a discussion on cell structure and types of cells. You may have wondered why we spend time on biology in water science, and as you found out, there are many reasons! For example, humans use microbes in water treatment facilities, employing these microscopic organisms to process the organic materials in the water. In another example, we also need to know about the cell structure and life cycle of microorganisms to find ways to treat water and remove any threats to human health.

Take some time to think about how significant bacteria, those common, primitive, unicellular organisms, are to human health. Bacteria have some unique characteristics like reproduction through binary fission, where an organism divides into two or more new organisms (Spellman, 2015).  Because of the various structural forms and components of different species, there is no typical bacterium cell structure to study and memorize. Instead, we review structural components such as capsules, cell wall, cytoplasm, flagella, inclusions, mesosome, nucleoid, plasma membrane, and ribosomes.

Several other microorganisms are also presented in Week 3, including protozoa, microscopic crustaceans, viruses, algae, and fungi. The final section explains some of the aspects of water treatment and pathogenic protozoa, with details on Giardia spp. and Cryptosporidium spp. Understanding how the details of their lifecycles are relevant to water treatment has really advanced the quality of municipal water supplies over the years.

Professor Reiss

******

Spellman, F.R. (2015). The science of water: Concepts and applications (3rd ed.). Boca Raton, FL: CRC Press.

Castaneda Topic 2: Yellowstone River Watershed Analysis
Contains unread posts
Abel Castaneda posted Oct 2, 2024 1:52 PM
Yellowstone River-Billings Watershed Analysis

For this weeks discussion, I opted to go over the Yellowstone River, flowing through Billings, Montana, which serves as a vital resource for the community. This watershed not only supports local agriculture and recreation but is also a key source of water for municipal and industrial uses. Despite its significance, the river faces ongoing environmental pressures that require immediate attention to ensure its long-term sustainability (EPA, 2023). A map of water monitoring locationsDescription automatically generated

Pollution Status and Sources of Impairment

As of the most recent EPA reports, the Yellowstone River near Billings is classified as impaired. A primary concern is nutrient pollution, with excessive nitrogen and phosphorus levels often traced back to agricultural runoff. These nutrients foster the growth of harmful algal blooms, which deplete oxygen levels and threaten aquatic ecosystems, though there were not any recently reported on the EPA site (EPA, 2021). Additionally, sedimentation—a result of erosion and deforestation—contributes to the degradation of water quality.

The river also faces contamination from coliform bacteria, including E. coli, I decided to specifically include this information as it was the topic I chose for last weeks case study. According to the National Park Service, the presence of E. coli in the Yellowstone River indicates contamination from human or animal sewage, which can pose serious public health risks, particularly for recreational users like swimmers and anglers (National Park Service, 2022). Urban runoff, aging sewage infrastructure, and agricultural waste are key contributors to this contamination, highlighting the need for more robust water management and sewage treatment systems. 

Major Threats to Water Quality and Availability

The Yellowstone River faces several critical threats. The most pressing of these is agricultural runoff, which introduces pollutants like pesticides, fertilizers, and animal waste into the watershed. These pollutants not only harm aquatic life but also pose challenges for water treatment facilities that supply drinking water to Billings residents (EPA, 2021).

Urban development in the region further compounds these issues. As Billings expands, so does the amount of impermeable surfaces like roads and parking lots. This increases the volume of stormwater runoff, which carries pollutants directly into the river. Without adequate stormwater management systems, this trend could worsen, particularly in the face of climate change, which is expected to alter precipitation patterns and exacerbate water scarcity during dry seasons.

A less discussed but equally significant threat is the impact of climate change. Reduced snowpack levels in the Rockies, which feed the Yellowstone River, could lead to lower water availability in the summer months. This would strain the balance between agricultural, industrial, and residential water use, possibly leading to conflicts over resource allocation (EPA, 2023).

Water Use and Its Societal Importance

The Yellowstone River supports multiple water uses, from agriculture to recreation. Billings relies heavily on this watershed for irrigation, which is vital to the local economy. In addition, the river’s waters are used for drinking, industrial processes, and recreation, including fishing and boating. The multi-use nature of the river presents a challenge in balancing ecological sustainability with human demands. Maintaining this balance will require not only improvements in water conservation practices but also innovations in wastewater treatment to ensure that pollutants are removed before they reach the river (EPA, 2021).

This multifaceted use of water also points to the importance of stakeholder collaboration. Farmers, urban planners, conservationists, and policymakers must work together to ensure that the river can continue to meet the needs of the community while protecting the ecosystem. For example, incentivizing farmers to adopt sustainable practices like buffer strips and cover crops could significantly reduce nutrient runoff and sedimentation.

Conclusion

Overall, the Yellowstone River near Billings is a critical watershed facing significant threats from both human activities and environmental changes. Addressing its pollution status will require a combination of regulatory enforcement, sustainable land use practices, and community engagement. While challenges remain, proactive measures can help preserve this vital resource for future generations.

References

U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2021). Waterbody report for yellowstone river - billings.     https://mywaterway.epa.gov/waterbody-report/MTDEQ/MT43F001_011/2021

U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2023). How’s my waterway: community overview for billings, mt 59107. https://mywaterway.epa.gov/community/59107/overview

U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2023). Water quality standards regulations: montana. https://www.epa.gov/wqs-tech/water-quality-standards-regulations-montana

Bailey Option 3 The Unnavigable Waters SCOTUS Vs WOTUS
Contains unread posts
Carrie Bailey posted Oct 2, 2024 11:57 PM
For this week&#x27;s discussion, I watched the livestream webinar on “Unnavigable Waters” The Supreme Court, and the scuttles of the Waters of the United States. 
It all started in 2007 when married couple Michael and Chantell Sackett wanted to develop a property close to a lake in the Idaho Panhandle. The Sackets began development, which involved filling the lake with gravel.  Neighbors began to complain because there was not a permit set in place and before you knew the EPA (Environmental Protection Agency) got involved and immediately halted construction after the determination that the lot the Sackets purchased was a federally protected wetland, which falls under the Clean Water Act.  Because the wetland was protected, the EPA ordered the Sackets to remove gravel and stop further construction. The Sackets fought through the court system for the next 14 years arguing that the wetlands that they filled were not protected under the “waters of the United States.”  Because of land in between and because there were other bodies of water around it.   The waters of the United States are defined as bodies of water that are found in the geographical regions of our nation.
The ruling of the Supreme Court was in a 5 to 4 favor of the Sackets, all 9 members of the court ruled that any body of water that does not connect to a body of water that is not federally protected does not have the same protection as others and would not meet the requirements of protection.  Because of this decision in favor of the Sackets, there has been a significant impact on wetland protection which in turn limits federal jurisdiction.   
The webinar goes on to state that this decision has created a major reaction among the stakeholders such as environmental groups, industries, and state agencies. And a setback for the protection of wetlands.

Week 5 topic 1/Chance Hicks
Contains unread posts
Chance Hicks posted Sep 30, 2024 12:22 PM
Hello class, Water pollution can be caused by many things. Luckly, I found a site discussing nine effective water pollution solutions for protecting the environment and would like to discuss a few possible solutions stated. Considered to be possibly the most effective effort, is water treatment in reducing water pollution. Before water is reintroduced into waterways, wastewater treatment facilities remove nearly all pollutants in wastewater by chemical, physical, or biological process (Sensorex, 2020). This action will remove harmful bacteria for the human body or can remove nutrients like nitrogen and phosphorus, which can cause harmful algae blooms in the environment. Another helper against water pollution is storm-water management. These flows of water can pick up harmful pollutants that will eventually reach natural bodies of water on their journey. To combat such issues, sand filtration and electrocoagulation to reverse osmosis and advanced oxidation have been implemented to reduce this kind of possible water pollution (Sensorex, 2020). Natural ecological practices can also benefit against water pollution. Such a practice is denitrification, which converts nitrates directly into nitrogen gas and helps to prevent nitrate contaminating the groundwater (Sensorex, 2020). In reducing the nitrate within the groundwater, algae and phytoplankton growth is hindered, protecting environmental processes. With practices like these protecting our water supplies, human health and the health of the natural environment can be ensured, and we will be able to look forward to a brighter future of clean water. What other efforts other than the ones stated draw your attention? I added in an image of the process of nitrogen being removed from the soil by denitrification to help with understanding. The website discussing denitrification is added as well.



Image: (Mukherjee, 2022)

Wagner - Topic 1 - Orphaned Wells
Contains unread posts
Gary Wagner posted Oct 2, 2024 10:30 PM
Good afternoon Class, 

For this week&#x27;s discussion I chose topic 1. A few weeks ago, I saw a news story about &quot;Orphaned or Zombie Wells&quot; (linked below). Before that I had never heard of the term, but it is well (oil or gas) that stopped producing enough material to be profitable so the company just walked away. Some companies did plug or cap the well when they left but prior to formal regulations companies were not legally required to properly plug or even clean up the well area. It is estimated that there were upwards of 1 million wells drilled before that legislation was established. If there is no company taking responsibility of the leaking well the burden falls onto state, local and even tribal governments to plug and secure. The orphaned wells are all across the United States from Pennsylvania to Texas to Alaska. These orphaned and polluting wells can be located anywhere, in populated areas, in water ways, and in remote areas as well, according to the &quot;Orphaned Wells Program Office&quot; 14 million Americans live within 1 mile of an orphaned well. Although the toxic and carcinogenic gases are a huge concern as well the improperly caps wells can release many contaminates into ground water as well. Oil, brine, and even radioactive materials can be released into water sources. It was in 2021 that an infrastructure law was passed that that provided $4.7 billion of federal funds to help correct the issues of orphaned wells.   

Video Zombie wells: The threat beneath - ABC News (go.com)

W5, George Steward: Addressing Water Pollution and Treatment Solutions through Soil Regeneration
George Steward posted Oct 5, 2024 6:51 PM•532 WordsLast edited: Saturday, October 5, 2024 6:59 PM EDT
Recently, I conducted the water test that I ordered through this course on my tap water, and the results were alarming. Living in the Edwards Plateau region of Georgetown, TX, we’ve always known that our water is hard due to the high levels of calcium carbonate from the limestone. However, the most concerning revelation was the potential presence of lead and pesticides in our water. This hits especially close to home for me and my family, as we eat organic food, grow as much as we can organically, and raise our animals as naturally as possible. To find out that these harmful chemicals have infiltrated our water supply is both disturbing and disheartening.

While we use a basic refrigerator water filter, it only addresses our drinking water. We are now researching whole-home filtration systems to ensure that the water we use for everything—drinking, bathing, cooking—is clean and free from contaminants. These systems, ranging from $2,000 to $10,000, could be a much-needed solution to safeguard our health. However, this situation has made me reflect on the root of the problem: pesticide runoff.

One of the key ways we can tackle pesticide runoff is by focusing on soil health, which is at the core of my work through my company. Healthy soil is the foundation for minimizing chemical runoff into water supplies. I have been working with biostimulants, which accelerate soil regeneration by improving microbial activity and overall soil structure. This promotes natural fertility, reducing the need for synthetic fertilizers and chemicals that contribute to water contamination.

Another effective solution is the use of biochar. Biochar is a type of charcoal that, when added to soil, helps lock in nutrients, improving water retention and keeping fertilizers and chemicals from running off into water systems. Biochar enhances the soil&#x27;s ability to support plant growth and it acts as a filter that traps harmful chemicals and prevents them from leaching into nearby water bodies.

By combining these two solutions—biostimulants and biochar—we can work toward regenerating the soil and reducing the reliance on harmful pesticides. This holistic approach helps protect water quality and helps build a more resilient ecosystem that supports long-term regeneration.

In a world where maintaining clean water seems increasingly difficult, focusing on the health of our soil offers a powerful and natural solution. By investing in soil regeneration, we can significantly reduce water pollution from pesticide runoff, improving both environmental and human health.

In the images below you will see the Earth Power Lodge where algae-based biostimulants are produced, a microscope image of biochar with a brief description of how it works, and a bottle of these two substances together. You are able to see the micronized biochar suspended in the biostimulant formula. My business efforts are focused on building a network of these production units that will be interconnected and producing the most optimal biostimulant formula for the environment where each unit is located. However, the current formula works very well for soil regeneration in many environments.

epl
biocharbiostimulant

References

NSF International. (n.d.). Home water treatment: Know your options. NSF. https://www.nsf.org/consumer-resources/articles/home-water-treatment

U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (n.d.). Safe Drinking Water Act (SDWA). EPA. https://www.epa.gov/sdwa

Georgetown Utilities. (2024). Water quality report for Georgetown, TX. Retrieved from https://georgetowntexas.gov/utilities/water/
